        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489"><meta property="og:title" content="Voltage-Modulated Structure Stress for Enhanced Electrochemcial Performances: The Case of μ-Sn in Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/asset/images/acs.nanolett.1c00489.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Alloy-type anodes in alkaline ion batteries have to face the challenges of huge volume change and giant structure strain/stress upon cycling. Here, reducing the structure stress for advanced performances by voltage regulation is demonstrated by using microsized Sn (μ-Sn) for sodium ion batteries as a model. Density functional theory and finite element analysis indicate that Sn/NaSn3 is the crucial phase transition highly responsible for inducing surface cracks, particle aggregations, and cell failures. Eliminating this phase transition by the control of cutoff voltages successfully extends the cycle life of μ-Sn to 2500 cycles at 2 A g–1, much longer than ∼40 cycles in a regular voltage window. The specific capacity is still retained at ∼455 mAh g–1, leading to a capacity decay of only ∼0.0088% per cycle. The results provide a simple way to achieve the outstanding performances without complicated preparation, expensive reagents, and laborious processing."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/asset/images/acs.nanolett.1c00489.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90da85e18cf1b325-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da85e18cf1b325-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '93d53e91-00b6-490a-810b-7df20521b366'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        <script async="" type="text/javascript" src="https://www.googletagservices.com/tag/js/gpt.js" rel="preconnect" nonce="90da85e18cf1b325-SJC"></script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da85e18cf1b325-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'nano_letters';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/nano_letters/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/nano_letters/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

title="Download Hi-Res Image" target="_blank" class="figure-viewer_hi-res-link"><i class="icon-file-image-o"></i><span>Download Hi-Res Image</span></a><span class="fv-separator"></span><a href="#" title="Download to MS-PowerPoint" class="figure-viewer_ms-p-link"><i class="icon-file-powerpoint-o"></i><span>Download to MS-PowerPoint</span></a><span class="cit-this-container"><a href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489&amp;href=/doi/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489" title="Cite This" class="figure-viewer-cite-this-link"><i class="icon-check-circle"></i><span><strong>Cite This:</strong></span></a><span class="article_header-cite-this figure-viewer-cite-this"><span class="cit-fg-title"><i>Nano Lett.</i></span><span <script nonce="90da85e18cf1b325-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "nalefd";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Voltage-Modulated Structure Stress for Enhanced Electrochemcial Performances: The Case of μ-Sn in Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Voltage-Modulated Structure Stress for Enhanced Electrochemcial Performances: The Case of μ-Sn in Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Voltage-Modulated Structure Stress for Enhanced Electrochemcial Performances: The Case of μ-Sn in Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Voltage-Modulated Structure Stress for Enhanced Electrochemcial Performances: The Case of μ-Sn in Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Voltage-Modulated Structure Stress for Enhanced Electrochemcial Performances: The Case of μ-Sn in Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Voltage-Modulated Structure Stress for Enhanced Electrochemcial Performances: The Case of μ-Sn in Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Voltage-Modulated Structure Stress for Enhanced Electrochemcial Performances: The Case of μ-Sn in Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Voltage-Modulated Structure Stress for Enhanced Electrochemcial Performances: The Case of μ-Sn in Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Voltage-Modulated Structure Stress for Enhanced Electrochemcial Performances: The Case of μ-Sn in Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

src="/products/achs/releasedAssets/images/orchid-2856f829046fbda55b90e1582edf0e9a.png" alt="Orcid"><a href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-8593-271X" title="Orcid class="loa-info hlFld-Affiliation"><div class="loa-info--wrapper"><div class="loa-info-name">Jian Yang</div><div class="loa-info-affiliations"><div class="loa-info-affiliations-info">Key Laboratory of Research Institute of Shandong University, Virtual University Park, Shenzhen 518057, China</div></div><span class="conrtib-corresp"><strong>*</strong>Email: <a href="mailto:yangjian@sdu.edu.cn">yangjian@sdu.edu.cn</a></span><span class="conrtib-corresp"></span><span class="conrtib-corresp"></span><div class="loa-info-scifinder"><span>More by <a href="/action/doSearch?field1=Contrib&amp;text1=Jian++Yang" data-id="article_author_search">Jian Yang</a></span></div><div class="loa-info-orcid"><img height="20" width="27" src="/products/achs/releasedAssets/images/orchid-2856f829046fbda55b90e1582edf0e9a.png" alt="Orcid"><a href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-6401-276X" title="Orcid class="article_rightPermissionsLink"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D3588%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DYansong%2BZhu%252C%2BZhao%2BQian%252C%2BJuan%2BSong%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D21%26issueNum%3D8%26contentID%3Dacs.nanolett.1c00489%26title%3DVoltage-Modulated%2BStructure%2BStress%2Bfor%2BEnhanced%2BElectrochemcial%2BPerformances%253A%2BThe%2BCase%2Bof%2B%25CE%25BC-Sn%2Bin%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1530-6984%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dnalefd%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D3595%26publicationDate%3DApril%2B2021" data-id="article_rights_permissions">Request reuse permissions</a></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_header-metrics-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="article_header-metrics-dropzone"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/asset/images/medium/nl1c00489_0006.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/asset/images/large/nl1c00489_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Alloy-type anodes in alkaline ion batteries have to face the challenges of huge volume change and giant structure strain/stress upon cycling. Here, reducing the structure stress for advanced performances by voltage regulation is demonstrated by using microsized Sn (μ-Sn) for sodium ion batteries as a model. Density functional theory and finite element analysis indicate that Sn/NaSn<sub>3</sub> is the crucial phase transition highly responsible for inducing surface cracks, particle aggregations, and cell failures. Eliminating this phase transition by the control of cutoff voltages successfully extends the cycle life of μ-Sn to 2500 cycles at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, much longer than ∼40 cycles in a regular voltage window. The specific capacity is still retained at ∼455 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, leading to a capacity decay of only ∼0.0088% per cycle. The results provide a simple way to achieve the outstanding performances without complicated preparation, expensive reagents, and laborious processing.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D3588%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DYansong%2BZhu%252C%2BZhao%2BQian%252C%2BJuan%2BSong%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D21%26issueNum%3D8%26contentID%3Dacs.nanolett.1c00489%26title%3DVoltage-Modulated%2BStructure%2BStress%2Bfor%2BEnhanced%2BElectrochemcial%2BPerformances%253A%2BThe%2BCase%2Bof%2B%25CE%25BC-Sn%2Bin%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1530-6984%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dnalefd%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D3595%26publicationDate%3DApril%2B2021" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2021 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Batteries" class="article__tags__link">Batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291445&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Chemical structure" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Chemical%20structure" class="article__tags__link">Chemical structure</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290776&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Phase transitions" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Phase%20transitions" class="article__tags__link">Phase transitions</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292548&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Stress" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Stress" class="article__tags__link">Stress</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Sodium-ion%20batteries">Sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Alloy-type+Anodes&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Alloy-type%20anodes">Alloy-type anodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Tin&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Tin">Tin</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Structure+Stress&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Structure%20stress">Structure stress</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Phase+Transition&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Phase%20transition">Phase transition</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><p class="first">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), as one of the promising next-generation batteries, arouses intensive interests both in industry and in academia, due to the high abundance of Na in earth and the similar underlying fundamentals to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> Moreover, the relatively high potential of Na<sup>+</sup>/Na with regard to Li<sup>+</sup>/Li (∼0.3 V) also potentially restrains the side reactions of electrolytes. However, the heavy ionic mass and large ionic radius of Na<sup>+</sup> retard its transportation in electrodes, thereby increasing the electrode polarization and lowering the electrochemical activity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 4 and 5">(4,5)</a> Therefore, it is challenging to obtain advanced electrode materials for SIBs. As far as anode materials are concerned, the promising candidates with industrial values are very limited, although there are already numerous reports in this field. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 6 to 10">(6−10)</a> Compared to hard carbon and red phosphorus, Sn-based composites might be the better choice as an advanced anode material, in view of its high specific capacity (∼847 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), the good electronic conductivity, and the high volumetric density (7.28 g cm<sup>–3</sup>), as well as the good safety under ambient conditions. Although Sn does not hold the advantages in all these terms, it is one of the best in overall performances. Therefore, the applications of Sn in SIBs attract widespread attention. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11 ref12 ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 11 to 13">(11−13)</a></p><p>Like other alloy-type anode materials, Sn-based composites also face the huge volume change and the destructive structure stress during the sodiation/desodiation processes, which then leads to the particle pulverization and the repeated growth of a solid-electrolyte-interphase (SEI) film. Therefore, the reversible capacity quickly degrades and the Coulombic efficiency sustains at a relatively low level. To address the issues, different strategies have been developed to alleviate the volume change and stabilize the electrolyte/electrode interface. The most popular strategies include the formation of carbon composites, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> and size control on the order of nanometers, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> as well as the generation of porous structures. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> Despite these strategies indeed improve the electrochemical performances, they induce other serious issues, such as low tapping density, low initial Coulombic efficiency, marked increase of production cost in time/materials, and so on. These issues can be avoided or alleviated by microsized particles, but the strategies that work for microsized particles (μ-Sn) are few, especially for solid powders. Recently, molecule engineering of electrolytes for advanced performances has been reported for anode-type anodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17 ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 17 and 18">(17,18)</a> Zhang and Zhu reported that the specific capacity of μ-Sn could be maintained at ∼600 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in ether-based electrolytes, even after 300 cycles at 0.5 C. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> The superior performances was ascribed to the ultrathin, NaF-rich, and robust SEI film, which offers high elasticity and good conductivity for stable cycling. Sun et al. thought that the anions in electrolyte salts changed the solvation sheath of Na<sup>+</sup>, then affecting the performances of μ-Sn. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> Compared to NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub> and NaClO<sub>4</sub>, NaPF<sub>6</sub> as the electrolyte salt led to the best result, ∼650 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. These results on μ-Sn are encouraging, but the performances are still far from satisfactory.</p><p>Here, reducing the volume change and the structure stress by adjusting the voltage window for advanced electrochemical performances is demonstrated using μ-Sn for Na storage as a model. It is well-known that Sn experiences a series of phase transitions during the sodiation/desodiation processes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> Because these phases are significantly different in crystal structures, huge volume change and giant structure strain/stress must be generated upon cycling, which then triggers the particle pulverization and the repeated growth of SEI films. If we carefully think about this complicate process, it can be easily concluded that the volume change and concurrent structure strain/stress during different phase transitions, i.e. Sn/NaSn<sub>3</sub>, NaSn<sub>3</sub>/NaSn, a-NaSn/Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>, and Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>/Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>, will not be exactly the same. Identifying the tough phase transition, which takes more responsibility to surface cracks and particle pulverization, and then removing it from the electrochemical reactions of Sn would be crucial to stable cycling. This deduction is conducted based on mechanical properties obtained by density functional theory (DFT) and structure stress calculated by finite element analysis (FEA). To our knowledge, the identification and the removal of the tough phase transition to promote cycling stability of alloy-type anodes are not reported before, but very useful.</p><p>Commercial Sn powders were used as the active material without any treatments in advance (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/suppl_file/nl1c00489_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). Then, they were ball-milled with acetylene black and polymer binder together in a regular way as the working electrodes. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a shows the typical voltage profiles of μ-Sn in the first cycle, well consistent with what documented in the previous works. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> The multiple voltage plateaus indicate that the electrode goes through a series of phase transitions during the discharge/charge processes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref19" aria-label="reference 16 and 19">(16,19)</a> In order to clarify which one makes the greatest damage to structure stability, volume changes and structure stresses related to these transitions are carefully checked. Because the lateral size of the electrode is almost fixed in coin cells, the electrode thickness is monitored as an indicator of volume change. As shown in the inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a, the initial electrode is densely packed by μ-Sn, acetylene black and polymer binder. As the sodiation process undergoes, the electrode thickness gradually increases from ∼14.7 to ∼43.1 μm. Mean-while, the electrode becomes loose and porous, although it does not pulverize into small pieces. These results indicate that the phase transitions induce great intrinsic strain/stress and alter the internal structure. If the electrode thickness is plotted against Na fraction in Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sn (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b), the curve shows a turning point at NaSn<sub>3</sub>, as highlighted by a red arrow. Without the compromise of conductive carbon and polymer binder, this abnormity at NaSn<sub>3</sub> would be more apparent in μ-Sn, as revealed by the calculated volume expansion based on the unit cells of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sn (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/suppl_file/nl1c00489_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>). This abnormity makes the first phase transition, i.e. Sn/NaSn<sub>3</sub>, different from the others, in view of the fast volume expansion.</p><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/asset/images/medium/nl1c00489_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/asset/images/large/nl1c00489_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Galvanostatic voltage profiles and (b, c) volume changes of μ-Sn in the first cycle. (inset) Cross-section SEM image of the electrodes at different states of discharge. (b) Electrode thickness vs <i>x</i> in Na<i><sub><i>x</i></sub></i>Sn. (c) Calculated volume expansion vs <i>x</i> in Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sn. The abnormality in Sn/NaSn<sub>3</sub> is highlighted by a red arrow.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/asset/images/large/nl1c00489_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>Then, the mechanical properties of the intermetallic phases are calculated in terms of elastic moduli. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21 ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 21 and 22">(21,22)</a> All the calculations were conducted within the framework of density functional theory (DFT). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a><a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/suppl_file/nl1c00489_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a> lists the bulk modulus (<i>B</i>), Young’s modulus (<i>E</i>), and shear modulus (<i>G</i>) of Na–Sn phases obtained by the Voigt–Reuss–Hill (VRH) approximation. The elastic moduli of pure Na and Sn are in good accordance with the reported data (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/suppl_file/nl1c00489_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22 ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 22 and 23">(22,23)</a> indicating the reliability and validity of our calculations. The intermetallic phases are constructed on the basis of Morris and Grey’s work, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> where NaSn<sub>3</sub>, <i>a</i>-NaSn, and Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> were identified by an <i>in-operando</i> pair distribution function (PDF), X-ray diffraction (XRD), and <sup>23</sup>Na/<sup>119</sup>Sn solid-state nuclear magnetic resonance (ssNMR). Our results suggest that even a small amount of sodiation in Sn leads to a marked decrease in elastic moduli (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a–c). Moreover, the elastic moduli are continuously reduced with the increase of Na content in Na–Sn, i.e. a typical elastic softening. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> The elastic softening increases the material ductility and the deformation capability, thereby benefiting the resistance to cracks and preserving the electrochemical activity. In all the phases, Sn presents the highest stiffness, suggesting that it is easier to crack as compared to the other intermetallic phases. In this context, the phase transition involving Sn should be avoided to improve the cycling performances, consistent with that hinted by the electrode thickness.</p><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/asset/images/medium/nl1c00489_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/asset/images/large/nl1c00489_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Mechanical properties of μ-Sn and intermetallic phases in sodiation/desodiation. (a–c) Elastic moduli vs <i>x</i> in Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sn. (a) Volume modulus (<i>B</i><sub>H</sub>). (b) Young’s modulus (<i>E</i><sub>H</sub>). (c) Shear modulus (<i>G</i><sub>H</sub>). (d) Electronic charge density distribution in Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sn. The purple isosurface is plotted at a density of 0.02 e<sup>–</sup>/bohr<sup>3</sup>. Cyan and gray spheres are Sn and Na atoms, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/asset/images/large/nl1c00489_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>In order to understand the elastic softening in-depth, the charge density distributions of μ-Sn and the intermetallic phases of Na–Sn are obtained by DFT calculations, as presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d. Before sodiation, the electronic orbitals spread over the neighboring Sn atoms, indicating a significant electron sharing between them. Therefore, there is a weakly covalent and directional nature in Sn–Sn bonds. In contrast, the electronic orbitals are almost localized over a small region in Na. On the other hand, the bond length of Sn–Sn is much smaller than that of Na–Na (Sn–Sn 3.02 Å vs Na–Na 3.64 Å). Moreover, there are four valence electrons in Sn but only one in Na. In this case, the delocalized electron distribution, the large number of valence electrons, and the short bond length make Sn–Sn stiffer than Na–Na, which well explains the large difference in elastic moduli between them. As the content of Na in Na–Sn increases, more and more Sn atoms are surrounded by Na atoms. Therefore, Sn–Sn bonds are gradually replaced by Na–Sn bonds. Then, the overlap of electronic orbitals becomes less, suggesting the increasing character of ionic bonds. Eventually in Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>, every Sn atom is surrounded by Na atoms, exhibiting a highly localized electron distribution as in the case of Na. Meanwhile, the bond length of Na–Sn (3.42 Å) increases, compared to that of Sn–Sn (3.02 Å). These changes in electronic structures induced by the gradual transition from Sn–Sn to Na–Sn leads to the observed elastic softening in Na–Sn upon sodiation, thereby preventing the particle pulverization.</p><p>With the calculated elastic moduli of the intermetallic phases, the structure stress during the phase transitions can be estimated by FEA. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> The geometry, mesh, and the involved material properties were provided in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/suppl_file/nl1c00489_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2 and Table S5</a>. Although Sn experiences multiple phase transitions in the sodiation process, each of them can be treated as the motion of a reaction front throughout the particle. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12 ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref20" aria-label="reference 12 and 20">(12,20)</a> All the Na concentrations, <i>c</i>, are normalized to that of Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>. First, the structure stress/strain during the phase transition of Sn/NaSn<sub>3</sub> is examined. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/suppl_file/nl1c00489_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a> shows the snapshots of Na distribution, Von Mises stress, and hoop stress, as the reaction front moves inward. Since the crack always occurs at the surface, the discussion focuses on the hoop stress at the surface. In the beginning (the sodiation depth, δ = 0.9), the sodiated shell expands in volume, resulting in a compressive hoop stress on the surface (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/suppl_file/nl1c00489_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3a</a>). As the sodiation goes (δ = 0.85 and δ = 0.6), the sodiated shell is gradually squeezed outward by internal expansion nearby the reaction front (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/suppl_file/nl1c00489_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3b and c</a>), inducing a tensile hoop stress on the surface. This tensile stress continuously accumulates and reverses the initial compression into tension, similar to the cases of Si. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 24 and 25">(24,25)</a> Second, the hoop stress at different phase transitions is compared at a sodiation depth of δ = 0.6, because only deep sodiation generates the hoop tension responsible for surface cracking. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 24 and 25">(24,25)</a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a> shows the distributions of Na content and hoop stress during different phase transitions, where Sn/NaSn<sub>3</sub> presents the largest hoop tension at the surface than the others. This result indicates that this phase transition has the highest possibility to induce the cracks, very consistent with the conclusion hinted at by volume change and DFT calculations. Meanwhile, it is noted that Sn/NaSn<sub>3</sub> also presents the largest hoop compression near the reaction front than the others. The drastic change from compressive stress to tensile stress within a limited distance easily induces the formation and propagation of cracks in particles.</p><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/asset/images/medium/nl1c00489_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/asset/images/large/nl1c00489_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Chemomechanical modeling of the distributions of Na content and hoop stress during different phase transitions: (a, e) Sn/NaSn<sub>3</sub>, (b, f) NaSn<sub>3</sub>/NaSn, (c, g) NaSn/Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>, and (d, h) Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>/Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>. To compare the simulations intuitively, the colorful maps are used.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/asset/images/large/nl1c00489_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>The direct demonstration of this conclusion comes with experiment results. The phase transition of Sn/NaSn<sub>3</sub> can be eliminated from the electrochemical reactions by lowering the upper limit of the voltage window to 0.62 V. Fortunately, the capacity contribution of this phase transition to Na storage is much smaller, as compared to the other phase transitions. To illustrate the that enhanced performance is not merely caused by reducing volume change, we also uplift the lower limit of the voltage window to 0.015 V as the benchmark, where the capacity from the phase transition of Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>/Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> is partially removed and the volume change is also reduced accordingly. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a–c shows the electrochemical performances of μ-Sn in different voltage windows (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), 0.01–1 V (regular window), 0.01–0.62 V (w/t Sn/NaSn<sub>3</sub>), and 0.015–1.0 V (less Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>/Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>). The electrodes are examined at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> for the first three cycles and then at 2.0 A g<sup>–1</sup> for the rest, where the reversible capacity decreases in all three cases at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The capacity decaying is most in the voltage window of 0.015–1.0 V. The capacity about ∼379 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> is much smaller than those of 0.01–1.0 V (∼652 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) and 0.01–0.62 V (∼583 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>). In theory, the less sodiation indicates the smaller volume change and the longer cycle life. However, as illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d, the cycling within 0.01–0.62 V exhibits the longest lifespan. After 2500 cycles at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, it still has a reversible capacity of 455 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to a capacity retention of 78% and a capacity decay of only ∼0.0088% per cycle. The cycling within 0.01–1.0 V dies only after 40 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/suppl_file/nl1c00489_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4a</a>). The case of 0.015–1.0 V survives ∼120 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/suppl_file/nl1c00489_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4b</a>), but the capacity continuously goes down to ∼275 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. These results indicate that the excellent performances in 0.01–0.62 V cannot be simply explained by the reduced volume change upon cycling, but closely related to the specific nature of intermetallic phases. Otherwise, the cycling within 0.015–1.0 V should present a better cycle life than that in 0.01–0.62 V. Even compared to nanoscale composites of Sn/C, this performance is still outstanding, as illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 12, 14 to 16 and 26 to 31">(12,14−16,26−31)</a> Beside the cycling performance, the rate performances in different voltage windows are also tested. As expected, the capacities in 0.01–0.62 and 0.015–1.0 V are less than that cycled in 0.01–1.0 V at the small current densities (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>f), due to the incomplete sodiation/desodiation process. However, at 3.0 and 4.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the capacity in 0.01–0.62 V becomes larger than the others, resulting in an improved capacity retention (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>g). The Nyquist plots at 0.27, 0.53, and 0.63 V show the close data in terms of charge-transfer resistance (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/suppl_file/nl1c00489_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>), excluding the effect from reaction kinetics. In this case, the capacity decaying in 0.015–1.0 V at high rates can be explained by the increased electrode overpotential that greatly limits the phase transition of Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>/Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> and reduces the specific capacity. The worse result in 0.01–1.0 V might be related to the reduced electrochemical stability, as indicated by <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d. The similar enhanced performances also exist in commercial powders without ball milling (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/suppl_file/nl1c00489_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S6 and S7</a>), confirming the validity of this strategy again.</p><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/asset/images/medium/nl1c00489_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/asset/images/large/nl1c00489_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Electrochemical performances of μ-Sn in SIBs. Galvanostatic voltage profiles in (a) 0.01–1, (b) 0.01–0.62, or (c) 0.015–1.0 V. These voltage profiles are obtained at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> in the 3rd cycle or at 2.0 A g<sup>–1</sup> in the 5th cycle. (d) Cycling performances, (f) rate performances, and (g) capacity retention of μ-Sn in different voltage windows. (e) Comparison of the reported data with ours.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/asset/images/large/nl1c00489_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>SEM images provide the direct evidence to support the improved structure stability by the removal of Sn/NaSn<sub>3</sub>. Before the cycling, the pristine electrode consists of a uniform mixture of μ-Sn, conductive carbon and polymer binder, as indicated by top-view and cross-section view of the electrodes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/suppl_file/nl1c00489_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>). μ-Sn is surrounded by tiny carbon particles, and the electrode is in good contact with Cu foil. After 30 cycles at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup> within 0.01–1.0 V, heavily aggregated particles dominate the electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a), leaving numerous voids, crevices, and gaps. Such a loose and mossy structure indicates the poor structure stability of μ-Sn in this voltage window. More worse, the electrode is detached from Cu foil, resulting in a poor electric connection and a reduced electrochemical activity. On the contrary, the electrode cycled in 0.01–0.62 V presents a relatively smooth and dense surface (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b), similar to the case of the pristine electrode. Meanwhile, the contact between the electrode and Cu foil is quite good. This contrast directly confirms that the removal of Sn/NaSn<sub>3</sub> from the electrochemical reactions of Sn significantly improves the structure stability. The electrode cycled in 0.015–1.0 V is just between the above two (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c). After 40 cycles, the cells cycled in 0.01–1.0 V die, due to severe electrode fracture and poor contact of electrode/Cu foil. Thus, we only examine the electrodes in 0.01–0.62 and 0.015–1.0 V with an extended cycle life. The electrode tested between 0.01 and 0.62 V remains almost unchanged after 120 cycles, although there is already electrode degradation to some extent (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d). The cracks inside the electrode start to show up, but do not propagate to the whole electrode yet. After 2500 cycles, the dense and solid particles of μ-Sn evolve into a walnutlike structure (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e). Although the particle size is basically maintained, the structure becomes porous. Fortunately, different domains in the walnutlike structure are well-connected, ensuring the good electrochemical activity. Such self-adapting can be considered as the mechanical response of the intermetallic phases to the electrochemically induced stress, based on their superior ductility and deformation capability. The case of 0.015–1.0 V is totally different. After 120 cycles, the signs of electrode failure, such as the huge cracks on the surface, the electrode detachment from current collector, etc., are observed in the electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>f).</p><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/asset/images/medium/nl1c00489_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/asset/images/large/nl1c00489_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. SEM images of the electrodes cycled in different voltage windows. (a–c) SEM images of the electrodes after 30 cycles in (a) 0.01–1.0, (b) 0.01–0.62, or (c) 0.015–1.0 V. (d, f) SEM images of the electrodes after 120 cycles in (d) 0.01–0.62 or (f) 0.015–1 V. (e) SEM images of a typical particle before and after 2500 cycles within 0.01–0.62 V.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/asset/images/large/nl1c00489_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>To demonstrate this strategy also works in full cells, homemade Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C (NVP@C) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/suppl_file/nl1c00489_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>) is taken to couple with μ-Sn. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/suppl_file/nl1c00489_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10b</a> displays the voltage profiles of Sn//NVP@C with and without the phase transition of Sn/NaSn<sub>3</sub>. As in the case in half cells, the cells cycled without the phase transition (2.75–3.8 V) present a smaller reversible capacity than those with the phase transition (2.50–3.8 V). The long and flat voltage plateaus at 3.3 and 3.1 V, higher than that of other cells, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10 ref11 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 10, 11 and 32 to 40">(10,11,32−40)</a> benefits the high-power applications (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/suppl_file/nl1c00489_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10c</a>). <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/suppl_file/nl1c00489_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10d</a> presents the cycling performances of Sn//NVP@C. After 100 cycles, the reversible capacity of the full cell cycled in 2.75–3.8 V is still ∼ 486 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, 87.4% of the capacity after electrochemical activation. In contrast, the reversible capacity tested in 2.50–3.8 V is almost ∼ 67 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 70 cycles, 11.2% of the capacity after electrochemical activation. Moreover, the rate performance in 2.75–3.8 V also shows the improved capacity retention and specific capacity at high rates (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/suppl_file/nl1c00489_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10e</a>), compared to the case in 2.50–3.8 V. Even at 4 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the cell still delivers a capacity of ∼351 mAh g<sub>anode</sub><sup>–1</sup>. Meanwhile that cycled in 2.5–3.8 V is only ∼70 mAh g<sub>anode</sub><sup>–1</sup>. In the Ragone plot (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/suppl_file/nl1c00489_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10f</a>), the energy density in 2.75–3.8 V remains at 112 Wh kg<sub>total</sub><sup>–1</sup> even at 1.39 kW kg<sub>total</sub><sup>–1</sup>. On the contrary, the energy density in 2.5–3.8 V falls to ∼16.5 Wh kg<sub>total</sub><sup>–1</sup> at 1.1 kW kg<sub>total</sub><sup>–1</sup>. The similar electrochemical improvement is also obtained for μ-Bi in potassium-ion batteries (PIBs) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489/suppl_file/nl1c00489_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>).</p><p class="last">In summary, microsized Sn is used as a model to identify a critical phase transition highly responsible for surface cracks and particle pulverization in SIBs. Based on the mechanical properties calculated by DFT and the structure stress simulated by FEA, the phase transition of Sn/NaSn<sub>3</sub>, which involves high-stiffness phases and large tensile hoop stress, is considered to be fatal to stable cycling. Thus, this phase transition is eliminated by controlling the voltage window. This modified voltage window (0.01–0.62 V) allows μ-Sn to exhibit a lifespan of ∼2500 cycles at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, much longer than ∼40 cycles tested within 0.01–1.0 V or ∼120 cycles within 0.015–1.0 V. After that, there is still a capacity of ∼455 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for μ-Sn, resulting in a capacity decay of only ∼0.0088% per cycle. The similar enhancement is also observed in full cells, using Sn//NVP@C as an example. More importantly, this strategy can be applied to other alloy materials, for example, μ-Bi in PIBs. The identification of the tough phase transition to promote cycling stability of alloy-type anodes is very useful. Moreover, this work provides a simple way to achieve outstanding electrochemical performance without expensive reagents, complicated preparation, and laborious processing.</p><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes2"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c00489</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">XRD patterns and SEM images of commercial μ-Sn and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion battery (SIB) as one of the most promising large-scale energy storage devices has drawn great attention in recent years.  However, the development of SIBs is limited by the lacking of proper anodes with long cycling life-spans and large reversible capacities.  Here the authors present rational synthesis of Rayleigh instability-induced Bi nanorods encapsulated in N-doped C nanotubes (Bi@N-C) using Bi2S3 nanobelts as the template for high-performance SIB.  The Bi@N-C electrode delivers superior Na storage performance in half cells, including a high specific capacity (410 mA h g-1 at 50 mA g-1), long cycling lifespan (1000 cycles), and superior rate capability (368 mA h g-1 at 2 A g-1).  When coupled with homemade Na3V2(PO4)3/C in full cells, this electrode also exhibits excellent performances with high power d. of 1190 W kg-1 and energy d. of 119 W h kg-1total. M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">β-SnSb for Sodium Ion Battery Anodes: Phase Transformations Responsible for Enhanced Cycling Stability Revealed by In Situ TEM</span>. <i>ACS Energy Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>3</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1670</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">1676</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acsenergylett.8b00762</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D3%26publication_year%3D2018%26pages%3D1670-1676%26journal%3DACS%2BEnergy%2BLett.%26author%3DH.%2BXie%26author%3DX.%2BTan%26author%3DE.%2BJ.%2BLuber%26author%3DB.%2BC.%2BOlsen%26author%3DW.%2BP.%2BKalisvaart%26author%3DK.%2BL.%2BJungjohann%26author%3DD.%2BMitlin%26author%3DJ.%2BM.%2BBuriak%26title%3D%25CE%25B2-SnSb%2Bfor%2BSodium%2BIon%2BBattery%2BAnodes%253A%2BPhase%2BTransformations%2BResponsible%2Bfor%2BEnhanced%2BCycling%2BStability%2BRevealed%2Bby%2BIn%2BSitu%2BTEM%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facsenergylett.8b00762&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.1c00489&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facsenergylett.8b00762&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">β-SnSb for sodium ion battery anodes: phase transformations responsible for enhanced cycling stability revealed by in situ TEM</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xie, Hezhen; Tan, Xuehai; Luber, Erik J.; Olsen, Brian C.; Kalisvaart, W. Peter; Jungjohann, Katherine L.; Mitlin, David; Buriak, Jillian M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Energy Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1670-1676</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AELCCP</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2380-8195</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">β-SnSb is known to be a highly stable anode for sodium ion batteries during cycling, but its sodiation-desodiation alloying reactions are poorly understood.  Combining in situ TEM with electroanal. methods, the authors demonstrate that β-SnSb forms Na3Sb and Na15Sn4 in sequence upon sodiation and re-forms as β-SnSb upon desodiation.  The neg. enthalpy of mixing for Sn and Sb is sufficient to cause sequentially deposited bilayers of Sn/Sb to transform into β-SnSb, resulting in comparable cycling stability.  The good cycling stability of β-SnSb results from the complex two-phase amorphous-nanocryst. microstructure in the partially charged-discharged states, as well as the intrinsic mech. toughness of the β phase.  Per the in situ TEM results, the sequential phase transformation shows minimal fracturing of the β-SnSb, indicating facile buffering of stresses.  Extensively cycled specimens eventually show cryst. Sn phase segregation, which may be the source of the ultimate capacity fade in the alloy and bilayers.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrS2jnkbSkBQ7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgGDXL5tAyiXw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXhtFGlsLfM&amp;md5=aeb83fa206cc39d9e748441f69f3b441</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1039/C8EE01023D"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zheng, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shao, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Z.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Recent Progress on Sodium Ion Batteries: Potential High-Performance Anodes</span>. <i>Energy Environ. Sci.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>11</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2310</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">2340</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Due to massively growing demand arising from energy storage systems, sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have been recognized as the most attractive alternative to the current commercialized lithium ion batteries (LIBs) owing to the wide availability and accessibility of sodium.  Unfortunately, the low energy d., inferior power d. and poor cycle life are still the main issues for SIBs in the current drive to push the entire technol. forward to meet the benchmark requirements for commercialization.  Over the past few years, tremendous efforts have been devoted to improving the performance of SIBs, in terms of higher energy d. and longer cycling lifespans, by optimizing the electrode structure or the electrolyte compn.  In particular, among the established anode systems, those materials, such as metals/alloys, phosphorus/phosphides, and metal oxides/sulfides/selenides, that typically deliver high theor. sodium-storage capacities have received growing interest and achieved significant progress.  Although some review articles on electrodes for SIBs have been published already, many new reports on these anode materials are constantly emerging, with more promising electrochem. performance achieved via novel structural design, surface modification, electrochem. performance testing techniques, etc.  So, we herein summarize the most recent developments on these high-performance anode materials for SIBs in this review.  Furthermore, the different reaction mechanisms, the challenges assocd. with these materials, and effective approaches to enhance performance are discussed.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Biomimetic Sn4P3 Anchored on Carbon Nanotubes as an Anode for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ran, Lingbing; Luo, Bin; Gentle, Ian R.; Lin, Tongen; Sun, Qiang; Li, Ming; Rana, Md Masud; Wang, Lianzhou; Knibbe, Ruth</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Recently, Sn4P3 has emerged as a promising anode for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to the high specific capacity.  However, the use of Sn4P3 has been impeded by capacity fade and an inferior rate performance.  Herein, a biomimetic heterostructure is reported by using a simple hydrothermal reaction followed by thermal treatment.  This bottlebrush-like structure consists of a stem-like carbon nanotube (CNT) as the electron expressway and mech. support; fructus-like Sn4P3 nanoparticles as the active material; and the permeable stoma-like thin carbon coating as the buffer layer.  Having benefited from the biomimetic structure, a superior electrochem. performance is obtained in the SIBs.  It exhibits a high capacity of 742 mA h g-1 after 150 cycles at 0.2C, and superior rate performance with 449 mA h g-1 at 2C after 500 cycles.  Moreover, the sodium storage mechanism is confirmed by cyclic voltammetry and ex situ X-ray diffraction and transmission electron microscopy.  In situ electrochem. impedance spectroscopy was adopted to analyze the reaction dynamics.  This research represents a further step toward figuring out the inferior electrochem. performance of other metal phosphide materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqi0vlL0Pw9D7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgGDXL5tAyiXw"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Building Fast Diffusion Channel by Constructing Metal Sulfide/Metal Selenide Heterostructures for High-Performance Sodium Ion Batteries Anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Tianshuai; Legut, Dominik; Fan, Yanchen; Qin, Jian; Li, Xifei; Zhang, Qianfan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
